Published by the Publishing Industry every Thursday.

Reprinting and Translation

— Adhering to Tradition First

According to Minister of Foreign Affairs Wang's own report to the Legislative Yuan, the Sino-American Treaty permits the translation of American publications but prohibits reprinting. Some people worry that even translation is prohibited. For example, according to one interpretation, if American works are registered or copyrighted with the Chinese authorities, then these publications should receive full protection from the Chinese government. We hope that the foreign affairs authorities will clarify this point.

However, we also feel that the prohibition of reprinting in the Sino-American Treaty is superfluous. China is a scientifically backward country, and the number of Chinese people who can read English books is already pitifully small. Even the number of people who can afford Chinese books is not very large. This restriction on reprinting does not benefit American publishers much, because the number of sales in China is very small. On the contrary, I think that scattering some pirated editions among Chinese bookstores can still have an advertising and promotional effect for the original authors. Furthermore, from the perspective of Chinese readers eager for Western knowledge, this only prohibits them from reading more Western books.

To be honest, if reprinting and translation were not restricted by the Sino-American Treaty and both parties were free to reprint and translate, it would benefit China, because judging from the current state of publishing in China and the United States, China could gain more from American works than the United States could gain from Chinese works. However, we must realize that American businesses have gained too much from the signing of this Sino-American Treaty. The United States is a powerful country, and even if it suffers a slight loss in this area, it can easily compensate for it in other areas, especially since this is greatly related to the exchange of Chinese and Western cultures!

This Sino-American Treaty seems equal on the surface, but it is unequal in essence. I think that what Mr. Sun Yat-sen distinguished between true equality and false equality can also be applied to this aspect. The United States is a country with a highly developed industry and commerce, while China is not. Therefore, if we say that China and the United States can freely navigate, it is equal on the surface, but in reality, the United States has ships, while China has no ships to navigate. Furthermore, if we say that China and the United States can freely trade, but more American goods are sold to China, while very few Chinese goods are sold to the United States, isn't this unequal?

If this is the case, why is navigation and trade open, while reprinting publications is prohibited? Chinese publishing circles only feel that our foreign affairs authorities have not considered the cultural future of China.

Reading "A Teacher's Diary"

— Yang Tongfang

Author: Feng Zikai Publisher: Wanguang Bookstore

Mr. Feng Zikai's "A Teacher's Diary" was published in Chongqing in June 1944, and re-published in Shanghai this June. A small part of it was published in various magazines in the rear areas between 1939 and 1940. Seven years ago, I received the "Cosmic Wind" magazine from a friend in Hong Kong and read an excerpt from "A Teacher's Diary," which was the first revelation. Since then, I had not seen it again.

From that first published diary entry, I glimpsed how an artist living a peaceful life abruptly left Yuan Yuan Hall and led his family on a difficult westward journey, and I felt deeply moved. Unfortunately, I was unable to see more records of his life after that. Now that Mr. Feng has published his diary, I spent two evenings reading it under the lamp, with the same feelings I had when I first read that diary. My emotions seemed to rise and fall with the author's emotions, and I was savoring the life of this artist.

In literary works, generally speaking, biographies and diaries are the most touching, because both are real records of the author's personal experiences. They can express the beauty of a person's individuality and personality, and among the two, diaries are the easiest to move people. Biographies are sometimes written by others, while diaries are always written by oneself. If we acknowledge the natural flexibility of short essays in expression, we will certainly find diaries even more endearing. The creation of short essays is not like grand treatises, not as serious and solemn as writing "great articles." Sometimes it is just about accidental feelings, fragmentary observations, with no attempt to meticulously describe the shape, thus it feels profound and interesting. Generally speaking, writing is rooted in nature, and what is inspired by the essence of one's spirit and what is revealed by practical experience must be more genuine and without pretense, natural and without carving. In this way, diaries are more in line with the conditions of natural expression. The diaries of various masters of the late Ming and Qing dynasties are still being recited by people, and there is a reason for this.

Like Mr. Zikai's diary, although it is only a thin one hundred and sixty pages, we can see from it the epitome of his wartime life and understand his usual life attitude. His heart is completely immersed in art, and even in the most difficult and disappointing situations, he still has a passionate heart full of hope. We all know how deep Mr. Feng's artistic attainments are. Artists have a sensitivity that surpasses ordinary people. They can truly appreciate and contemplate everything in the world and restore its original appearance, without being blinded by the various temptations and prejudices of the outside world. Therefore, they can shorten the distance between people and achieve the leisurely realm of "observing all things quietly and naturally." The reason why human beings have hatred, suspicion, and jealousy is that the boundaries between people have not been broken. The "self-image" and "self-attachment" mentioned by Buddhists are indeed the source of stinginess and greed. After reading "A Teacher's Diary," we will definitely be able to appreciate how noble and precious the mind of an enlightened artist is.

This "A Teacher's Diary," as the name implies, records his teaching career. The content includes the author's entire life teaching at Guilin Art Normal School after his westward journey, and then continuing to teach at Zhejiang University after leaving Guilin Art Normal School. A part of this can also be seen in the diary. The author taught at Guilin Art Normal School and Zhejiang University, completely adopting an enlightening method of teaching that did not focus on rote learning. What he was responsible for was the art department. He did not focus on imitation and copying, but on stimulating students' creative spirit and improving students' appreciation ability. What was especially important was to connect art with real life. The author believes that to achieve the purpose of art education, the entire life must be permeated with artistic elements to realize "artistic life" and "life artistized." Indeed, art cannot be isolated. Art that is isolated from real life cannot be called art. The author also wants us to open up another boundary outside the boundaries of reason and pragmatism, then "the world is always new, and everywhere is a beautiful boundary." How can this realm be appreciated by those who are busy with fame and fortune and enslaved by material desires?

After reading "A Teacher's Diary," we can know that Mr. Feng is not only an artist but also an ideal educator. His diary is full of love for young people. In addition to paying attention to teaching materials and teaching methods, teachers also need to have a loving heart. If teachers cannot sincerely love students and cannot use passionate love to nourish students' minds, then they will certainly not achieve the effect of education. Mr. Feng's every word and deed to his students often revealed a deep love in individual conversations and accidental encounters. He is worthy of being a "teacher," using his personality to influence his students. That kind of care and concern, just like treating his own children, can be said to be completely from the heart, without any pretense, especially in the details. I think the author was influenced by Mr. Li Shutong (i.e., Master Hongyi) and Mr. Xia Gengzun, because these two were Mr. Feng's teachers, and he had been influenced by their personalities.

The diary writes the most about the scenes of the war, with records of heroic deeds, exposures of the enemy's atrocities, inspiring words, and feelings from the depths of the heart. Although sometimes it is inevitable to be indignant and lament, he never despaired or declined. He firmly believes that China's War of Resistance will be victorious, because he sees thousands of young people growing up in the War of Resistance, and thousands of descendants of the Yellow Emperor fighting with their blood and flesh in the torrent of the war of resisting aggression. His optimistic spirit about the War of Resistance is as admirable as his tireless spirit of teaching people. Now that the War of Resistance has been won, his words may seem meaningless, but in fact, it was very difficult for him to maintain such a belief in victory at that time. Especially after each setback and damage (such as the Great Bombing of Guilin), he did not show any decline or disappointment. Speaking of the hardships of wartime life, he always says "good fortune comes from misfortune," and in fact, this War of Resistance is an opportunity for the rebirth of China.

In this diary, the lines are also filled with a strong human touch. At a glance, one can see the depth of the author's artistic cultivation. Even a small thing in life can be given an artistic perspective, and the trivial matters around him become a valuable source of life. Occasional small talks can set off endless deep meaning. A kind of gentle demeanor of the benevolent is the fusion of Confucianism and Taoism, and it is expressed by deeply understanding the essence of art.

There are also many records of exchanges with writers. The most frequently mentioned in the diary are Chuan Binran, Bing Xin, Song Yunbin, Tang Xianzhi, Xia Gengzun, and Master Hongyi. His wife, Li Min, and his children, Chen Bao, Yan Zhan, Ci Ying, Yi Yin, and his newborn son, Xin Mei, are even more frequently mentioned in the diary. The harmonious and warm atmosphere in the family indeed nourished the author's lonely heart during his escape. Family jokes, which ordinary people find meaningless, become moving descriptions under Mr. Zikai's pen. If it were not for an artist with unique insights, who would appreciate the beauty of life and enjoy the pleasure of being with family and children in the tense life of the war?

The conversations of rural women and wild people, and the songs of cowherds and woodcutters fully express the nature and simplicity of agricultural society; and the author's life is permeated with these elements. At the same time, the author also understands the true meaning of the philosophy of life. He has a vitality that is full in his thoughts, so what he says, even if it is very ordinary, can also give people a profound and fresh feeling.

Anyone who has read Mr. Zikai's "Yuan Yuan Hall Essays" and "Yuan Yuan Hall Re-Essays" will definitely understand his attitude towards life. We always feel that his essays have a simple and distant artistic conception. He sees all things in the universe, and none is not beautiful. He feels that all things in the world are worthy of people's appreciation and understanding. In his diary, we can also see this attitude towards life. Also, his writing is relaxed and lively, without a trace of carving. This is not so much the naturalness of his writing as it is the simplicity of his character. Recently, he has published a collection of essays entitled "Simplicity Collection." I think the reason why Mr. Zikai's life is so desirable is because of his simplicity.

Because his life is natural and simple, his diary contains a strong sense of humor, both solemn and humorous, which is quite fascinating. His writing does not have unique and wonderful places, but in the ordinary, it can show unique and wonderful things. He has already achieved the point of imparting great principles in simplicity, imparting world affairs in satire, and finding pure and beautiful charm in lightness. This may be the reason why Mr. Zikai's diary can arouse the love of most people?

Mr. Zikai's cartoons are unique, with the advantage of clear lines and realistic expression. I think his diary also has this characteristic.

A General Discussion of Chinese Historiography

— Written by Mu

Author: Zhu Xizu Publisher: Independent Publishing House

A general understanding of the various systems of Chinese historiography is essential for every friend who loves history. Young people, in general, must understand the developmental process of history, and they should also pay attention to the development of historiography. This is because historical records are not necessarily reliable. Throughout history, most have been "official histories" based on imperial chronicles, and these records inevitably contain inaccuracies. Understanding the development of historiography allows one to distinguish between truth and falsehood, providing a great deal of help in reading history.

This book, "A General Discussion of Chinese Historiography," is based on the author's lecture notes from the Department of History at Peking University, compiled by his son-in-law, Luo Xianglin. It provides us with a concise overview of Chinese historiography, allowing us to understand the development of Chinese historiography throughout history.

First, the author discusses the origin of Chinese historiography. Regarding the basic meaning of the word "史" (history), the "Shuowen Jiezi" (Explaining and Analyzing Characters) says, "史 is one who records events. It follows '又' (hand) holding '中' (middle), meaning impartiality." The author provides a brief explanation and examples of its use. He then explains that history includes written records, records kept by scribes, falsely attributed records, and history inferred from ancient artifacts before the advent of writing. He discusses the advantages and disadvantages of these various historical systems, which were not called "history" in the old days. The "history" of that time was merely the not-very-accurate "official history" produced by scribes, which gradually developed and nurtured the seeds of history.

Regarding the schools of Chinese historiography, the author discusses six schools, spanning from the Spring and Autumn period to the present day. These six schools are as follows:

(1) Annals: Distinguished by time, also known as chronological history. This type of history began after the Western Zhou dynasty. The era before the Western Zhou is unclear, so this system did not exist before that time. Common examples of this type of history include comprehensive works such as the "Zizhi Tongjian" (Comprehensive Mirror to Aid in Government) and individual works such as the "Spring and Autumn Annals."

(2) National Histories: Distinguished by place, also known as local history. This type of history collects and examines the history of various ancient states. The ancient states, both large and small, cannot be simplified. Comprehensive examples include the "Records of the Three Kingdoms," and individual examples include the "Chronicles of Huayang."

(3) Biographies: Distinguished by people. Within these biographies, "one can glimpse the level of civilization in a place and the gains and losses of society." Common examples include "Biographies of Women" and "Unofficial Biography of Dongfang Shuo."

(4) Political History and Cultural History: Distinguished by subject. Political history includes economic history, legal history, military and political history, social party history, and diplomatic history. Cultural history includes academics, religion, literature, art, agriculture, industry, commerce, customs, and other items. Examples of the former include the "Tongdian" (Comprehensive Statutes) and "Tongkao" (Comprehensive Examination of Institutions), and the "Tang Liudian" (Six Statutes of the Tang Dynasty). Examples of the latter include "Bielu" (Separate Records), "Qilue" (Seven Summaries), "Song Yuan Xue'an" (Case Studies of Song and Yuan Scholars), and "Poetry Criticism," etc.

(5) Official History: The so-called official history is not written by one person at one time, but is a "official history" accumulated over generations, such as the Twenty-Four Histories.

(6) Chronicle of Events from Beginning to End: Histories written about the beginning and end of an event. This system was created by Yuan Shu of the Song Dynasty. Examples include "Chronicle of Events from Beginning to End of the Comprehensive Mirror," "Chronicle of Events from Beginning to End of the Three Feudatories," and "Chronicle of Events from Beginning to End of Western Xia."

The above six types are only a brief outline. The book discusses them in detail in separate sections.

There are two appendices. One explains "Taishigong Jie" (Understanding the Grand Historian), elaborating on Sima Qian's writing of history. It is best to refer to Jian Bozan's "Historical Materials and Historiography," specifically the article "Sima Qian's Historiography," for a more detailed understanding. The other appendix is an examination of the compilation of annals for the twelve emperors of the Han Dynasty, from Emperor Gaozu of Han, through Emperor Hui, Empress Lü, Emperor Wen, Emperor Jing, Emperor Wu, Emperor Zhao, Emperor Xuan, Emperor Yuan, Emperor Cheng, Emperor Ai to Emperor Ping. The author describes and lists these twelve emperors' annals, providing a clear and precise indication of the same.

Although this book is concise, its content is quite extensive. Young friends who are just beginning to study history can use it as a good reference. Even if there are omissions, they can still be found, and at this time, it can still serve as a reference for general beginners. The book also points out many errors in historiography.

Today, everyone knows that history is an important discipline in the social sciences. It can't be ignored by every young person. Before reading history, we must understand the various branches of Chinese historiography, so that we can get a lot of convenience when reading history.

This book can serve as a preparation before reading history, and it can also serve as a reference after studying history.

The Devil's Pool

— Xu Peiji

Behind the easy and frank narrative, there is a hidden, refined, and elegant charm; this is a unique style of pastoral novels. If you compare the scenery of nature to a small stream formed by mountain springs, although it is a dwindling stream, the gurgling sound of the water still echoes intermittently in the winding cliffs. In the clear riverbed, the gently undulating wave marks on the water surface clearly show the potential of life, constantly jumping there. George Sand's "The Devil's Pool" depicts a simple and unadorned story of a French rural village. After getting used to the magnificent works that reflect the era, it is like drinking a cool drink in the scorching heat; it is like the early autumn, bathing in the warmth of the morning sun in the slightly chilly breeze.

Ah! The development of the story is as simple, straight, and unadorned as the ditch that a farmer plows with his cattle. This ditch may soon be submerged by a second ditch in the vast wilderness, but under the author's pen, the vivid and skillful narrative will forever keep a deep imprint of it in the garden of life.

Look, the typical old farmer of a hundred years ago - the rational Father Maurice - he consoles his son-in-law, he covers up his grief over the loss of his beloved daughter, and advises his young son-in-law to remarry without jealousy. But young and handsome Jeannot, the faithful protagonist of the book, with his full enthusiasm, cherishes the memory of his deceased wife, and in front of his lively children, he is a considerate and loving father. In the village of Bérelles, his sincerity is loved by all the residents, and when he is fascinated by every move of little Marie, he is as foolish as an ignorant child. But that foolishness, in certain situations, can lead to his courageous decisiveness. When he stepped into the house of the widow Guillette, whom his father-in-law, Father Maurice, pointed out to him as a potential wife, he immediately saw the different natures of exaggeration, vanity, and glory. He cleverly withdrew from the false trap. The author does not directly describe the appearance of little Marie, who is innocent and intelligent, but only reflects her inner response through her words and deeds. Her candor and purity, compared with the vanity and affectation of the widow Guillette, is a world apart. Tender, clever, and a daughter of nature, she moved Jeannot, who was approaching his thirties. She won Father Maurice's approval, and she caressed Jeannot's son - little Pierre, who was not yet seven years old. In her eyes, there is no difference between the "love" she receives from others and the "love" she gives to others. But those who receive her "love" clearly feel that there are different weights! At the end of the story, Jeannot, with a near-desperate mood, walks into the house of Mother Guillette - little Marie's mother - to ask little Marie for a final decision on "love." She cannot help but exclaim, "Didn't you feel that I loved you before?" Ah! The reader's heart will also be moved by her frank exclamation. We can easily imagine what an enviable comedy will follow.

In the course of the story, the author uses her skillful technique to weave little Pierre's charming language into the important joints of the whole book, which is extraordinarily appropriate and flavorful. She also depicts the wickedness of Aumord, the landlord. When he was oppressing little Marie and Jeannot's way of punishing him was only to drag him off his horse and press him to the ground; from the author's compassionate heart, we can see the author's view of life and the attitude towards art.

"The artist's faithful portrayal of the society in which he lives is a painful satire. He focuses on sin and suffering; but what should we, the later artists, depict? We will wait for the expectation of seeing death, which is the reward of mankind today, and we will pray for it to be a punishment for evil and a compensation for disaster?"

In George Sand's time in Europe, most art workers were accustomed to revealing the dark side of society and injecting gloomy satire into their works. Dürer (Albrecbt Durct), Michelangelo (Michaelangelo), Holbein (Holbeih), Callot (Callot), and Goya (Goya) strongly satirized the evils of their countries, and their immortal works are historical documents with positive value. We will not deny artists the right to explore and expose the scars of society; but is exposure the only function of art?

Based on her true heart, the author recognized these vulgar and cruel views. She believed that the mission of art is a mission of emotion and love, and that artists should undertake a major poetic work - "We would rather take mild and gentle characters that can be reformed than frightening villains that cause terror, because terror will never correct selfishness, but will only increase it." After affirming his artistic point of view, she issued an ode to "life" - a view of life and the universe.

"We should pay attention to life, not death. We no longer trust the emptiness of the tomb... To live richly, we must appreciate life. Lazarus should leave his dunghill so that people will no longer rejoice in the death of the rich. Everyone should promote happiness, so that the good fortune of a few will not lead to sin and suffering... We can no longer see death as a punishment for prosperity or a comfort for disaster... We will no longer allow any tomb to be the only escape for those whom we do not wish to make happy."

Using her correct thinking to measure the negative attitude held by most people in today's society and the key to happiness that they have abandoned, it is so regrettable.

Writing this, we ask to close the book and meditate on the voices and smiles of the characters in the book, the lively and moving atmosphere, which contains a flavor as fragrant and timeless as an olive. The end of the story is as bright as the sunset after the wind has passed.

Page 11

Spring and Autumn

My Recent Life and Writing

— Lu

Preface

It was on the afternoon of the 17th of this month at the literary tea party that, due to everyone's temporary interest, I proposed this topic, asking the friends who came to participate to write a little about the trivia around them. The intention was nothing more than to give readers a better understanding of the writers and to add some interest to the publication.

Thanks to the friends who participated that day for not being offended by my method of "assigning a topic and submitting the paper on the spot," everyone put aside their teacups and wrote it in one go. A total of thirty-eight pieces were received at that time, and there were still those who accepted the topic and were expected to submit their papers later.

Now, according to the order in which they were received, the first ten copies that have been sorted out will be published. However, due to the limitation of space, the rest and those received successively will be prepared for publication in the next week. I hope my friends will forgive me. ——Editor

One

Writing About the Tea Party

The tea is still bitter, and will always be bitter, and it is precisely because of this bitterness that we cannot forget it. Talking in the bitterness makes one feel refreshed. What's more, the objects of the conversation are all like-minded people who know the bitterness, so they are not afraid of talking too much, and do not distinguish the bitterness of the tea.

Two

My recent life consists of nothing more than teaching, editing, and writing. Every Monday, Wednesday, and Friday, I teach "Selections of Qu" (Chinese Operatic Songs), "History of Chinese Novels," and "Chinese Language and Literature Department Composition" at the Department of Chinese Language and Literature at National Fudan University in Jiangwan. On Tuesday afternoons, I teach "History of Chinese Drama" at the Municipal Experimental Drama School.

I am the chief editor at Beixin Bookstore, editing the monthly magazine "Youth World." This publication has a history of more than ten years and is a comprehensive publication aimed at middle school and university students. Before the war, its sales reached tens of thousands of copies. It is currently being published as Volume 2, Number 3 of the new series. In addition, I publish the "Popular Literature" weekly in the Da Wan Bao every Tuesday, and the "Vulgar Literature" weekly in the Central Daily News every Thursday. Mr. Dai Wangshu and Mr. Ye Dejun, who write for the Spring and Autumn section of this newspaper, are the basic contributors to these two publications. Researching Chinese novels and dramas has almost become my addiction.

Because life is busy, and there is no whole block of time, I cannot write a systematic and complete work, but only write sporadic articles for various publications, but I am always struggling to cope, and I feel like I have nothing to say. Most of what I write is textual research on novels and dramas, and occasionally I also write a little about my reading and viewing experiences. All publications hope that I will write literary memoirs, but I have already published "Literary Profiles" and "Literary Impressions." Although I have met many new literary people, I do not have a deep friendship with them. The biggest regret is that I do not have time to read each writer's works carefully, so what I force myself to write is not profound. I think writing this kind of article is the most old-fashioned thing.

Three

I'm afraid that I won't be able to write articles if I don't have enough to eat.

Four

When Jinshan came to collect articles, the threshold was too high. As soon as I sat down, before I could talk to him, he gave me a topic and asked me to submit the paper, which is the way to test new students. It's really a bit overwhelming. Fortunately, there is no limit to the number of words or the style, so I will "take the exam" once.

I came from Hankou this time and passed through Shanghai to Peiping. Since the sixth of this month until today, I have been living a traveling life, so I am very unstable. But it is a very happy thing to be able to meet friends that I haven't met in more than ten or even twenty years!

As for writing, I am very ashamed. The "Victory Travelogue" that I plan to write has just begun. After I get settled in Peiping, I plan to finish writing the two long novels, "Great Fire's Woman" and "In the Flames of War." In addition, I want to read more books.

I have been away from Shanghai for more than nine years. It really makes me very happy to be able to meet many old friends today. I want to talk, so I will stop writing here.

Five

Recently, I returned to my hometown, which I had not returned to for ten years. After returning to Shanghai, I originally wanted to write some essays like reflections to give to Jinshan. It happened that the Noon News was published, and the editor asked me to continue writing "Baby Diary." So I changed my plan, but after writing a few paragraphs, the baby became spoiled and refused to write anymore. Coincidentally, Bing Xin came from Hankou. She wanted to go to Peiping to edit the monthly magazine "Literature and Life" and asked me for "literary works." I thought I would give her this unfinished Baby Diary, but the baby's age is too young, and I am afraid that she will not be able to withstand the hardship of the journey. Moreover, the season has entered winter, and Peiping is freezing cold, and I am even more afraid that my baby will not be able to bear it; so I had to think of another way.

Six

Speaking of my life, it is really not worth mentioning. I remember that in the 20s, when I was studying at school, I read the debate on the scientific view of life, in which Mr. Wu Zhihui's view of life was three items: "eating, having sons, and entertaining friends." In the past twenty years, my life has been nothing more than these three items; in my recent life, except for the second item, which I am not prepared to expand, the third item has always been the same, and every time I decide to participate in a literary tea party, I just want to greet more friends.

Speaking of writing, I am very ashamed. I am busy every day to eat, working as a lawyer and doing business at the same time. I really don't often write. If the editor wants me to write an article, of course I dare not refuse.

Seven

Recently, in addition to handling fixed routine official business every day, in my spare time, I also took on the special writing of two or three publications, that is, I am busy all day long. I don't have a share in the dance hall, and at most I just watch movies. I don't drink alcohol or smoke cigarettes, and this has been the case for more than thirty years. In addition to writing, reading books and newspapers are also things that I must do every day.

Eight

Now I am busy giving lessons at the Shangchuan, Chengming, and Meizhuan schools, rushing to meet the time, and squeezing onto the bus. It can be said that "there is no time to rest all day long"; therefore, the effort of writing is also very minimal. Before the summer vacation, I still painted every day, but now I can only paint one or two paintings a week, and I can barely write one or two short articles; as for the "Great Dictionary of Names of Chinese Painters" that I planned to add to and revise, and the "Great Collection of Chinese Calligraphy and Painting of Past Dynasties" that I compiled, I cannot proceed with them, and I can only wait until life is a little more leisurely before I can study them carefully.

Nine

Life is dull, but there is poetry to prove it:

"Everything seen must be recorded, everything heard must be written, there is no holiday in the four seasons, when will it rest? Long gatherings only teach estranged friendship, but at the parting, I compose a farewell sorrow!" (First)

"Spring flowers and autumn moons pass by casually, snails compete with roc, and all are in the net, but I hate that the pen is bald for a long time, what to do with deleting poems, books, and history!" (Second)

"Dancing like a startled swan, graceful like a dragon, floating clouds and world affairs are the same in the nine provinces, please enter the jar, don't laugh at me, I am also confused in the painting." (Third)

Ten

Before the Anti-Japanese War, I spent my life with chalk, was trapped in Jiangnan, and participated in guerrilla work, suffering from wind and frost. After the victory, I became a civil servant for the first time, perhaps luckily, and the job was very leisurely, so the work of reading and writing was openly carried out at the desk. After Xiao Qian returned to China, he was in charge of the literary compilation of the Dagong Newspaper, and asked me to write book reviews for "Literature" regularly. Living in an unstable place and a turbulent life, it is inconvenient to collect many reference books, and it is not easy to search and purchase them. I know that I cannot give many examples in my writing; writing a book review cannot discuss the entire creative process of the author in detail, which makes me feel uneasy and embarrassed in my heart.

Eleven

I am a current primary school teacher. Speaking of primary school education, it should be so important, and those who are engaged in education should dedicate all their energy and all their time to our later generations. But now is an era when eight or nine out of ten things are unsatisfactory, and cultural people naturally cannot forget about literature and art.

I am currently writing short stories, but this era often cannot complete a work as you wish. Of course, there are many reasons for this, the most important of which is the writing environment. In an atmosphere where the whole life cannot be stable, the creation of literary works is difficult.

Twelve

Speaking of life, it seems that recently it is the same as in the past, and there is no change. The week before last, I went home and spent some more leisurely life, appreciating some of the mountains in late autumn, and tasting the flavor of Wang Si Restaurant's "Oiled Chicken" that I had not tasted for eight years.

Recently, my "Four Great Painters of the Ming Dynasty" has been completely arranged and proofread by World Publishing Company, and "Research on Color Science" has been contracted with me by the Commercial Press for royalties. Therefore, it aroused my interest in writing long articles again, and I prepared to complete a 30,000-word "A Brief History of Emerging Chinese Art" before the end of the year. I really want to write it from a "materialist" point of view. This is an attempt. I don't know if I will be able to write it.

Thirteen

This semester, I took classes at Dongyi University and the Provincial Suzhou Industrial Vocational School, etc., plus the various things in the local area, I was so busy that I had no time to read. Recently, I came to Shanghai, and although I am still busy, it is much better. Every one or two weeks, I return to Suzhou, and sometimes I go to Hangzhou, and my life has added a lot of interest. Stealing leisure from busyness, I really want to read some famous works of classical literature. For the time being, I don't want to write, what I want to prepare is to write "A General History of China," but this is not a casual matter, and I don't dare to say now that I will definitely start writing.

Fourteen

Recently, in my spare time at the bank, because I am the chief editor of the local newspaper "Dong Bao" in Pudong, it has affected the time for creating. I hope that I have some time to sort out the manuscripts of "Special Ethnic Groups in Guangxi" first, and then write the two books "Research on Pangu" and "Exploration of Flood Legends and the Myth of Fuxi Creating Humans." As for the outlines of the two books "Collection of Customs in the Southwest" and "Collection of Drama Criticism" have been roughly completed, and if there is an opportunity for publication, they can be sorted out and sent to print immediately.

Fifteen

Mr. Jinshan gave the topic and ordered, "Submit the paper on the spot!" Although the brain juice is dry, and I twist it horizontally and vertically, I can't squeeze out a drop. But the order to submit the paper on the spot, ah! Dare not obey!

In the past, life was often, perhaps because I was a bachelor, I had wine, I got drunk; I had money, I used it; I had time, I played; it was a lot of fun! Walking at night and swimming in the garden in the rain were commonplace. Perhaps it was because of the war that it seemed impossible for a person's temperament to be calm in a chaotic world.

Recently, I have changed. To quote a literary buzzword, I have changed my style. I'm sorry, it's not because I have a wife. (My wife is a "rat" from Hedong, but she can't roar like a lion, she's really a stupid donkey!) However, I ask myself that life should be organized. Perseverance and hard work require determination.

Therefore, recently I have been determined to try the tempering of life!

Recently, although I really want to write, I lack the childish naivety and the spirit of hard work. What I lack even more is trust in myself. Except for occasional translations, I really don't dare to write. I really don't dare to write.

Sixteen

I am a news practitioner. During the Anti-Japanese War, I worked in the rear newspaper industry. Because I had lived in the north for a long time, I was not accustomed to the life in the southwest, and the friends in the rear were gradually leaving, giving me a feeling of living alone! So I left Jincheng on October 21 and came to Nanjing and Shanghai to see friends I hadn't seen for a long time. In two or three days, I will go to the old capital I haven't seen for a long time to engage in the news business.

Seventeen

For eight years, I lingered in Shanghai, which was shrouded in demonic atmosphere, and I did not act as a traitor. I only closed my doors and painted all year round; sometimes I wrote a few short poems to express my feelings. Many friends thought that I, who was as poor as I am, must have starved to death; friends who cared about me asked me about my recent life, and I only asked him to look at the couplet I wrote in my studio, which read: "Read books for half a day, paint for half a day. Sometimes grow vegetables, sometimes plant flowers." This is a realistic description of my life, but I feel that I have accomplished nothing, and I am getting old, and I feel very sorry for my friends who care about me.

Eighteen

Everyone knows that running news is the most free job. It is certainly enviable to be able to develop freely without constraints. However, there are also very brain-consuming aspects to entering this circle. Every day, I chase clues, rush to manage time, regardless of day and night, regardless of distance, and drill and interview everywhere. I live in this situation every day. But I am very happy, I find it very interesting, and my mood is happy.

Because I focus on news reports, I have inadvertently become indifferent to literary writing. But it is not an abandonment, it is not a disgust, but in reality, it is the lack of time, so in addition to news articles, and writing some news features, recently I can say that I have not written anything else.

Nineteen

Because I participated in the Anti-Japanese War, I joined the Air Force. Years of mechanical life made me feel bored, so in order to adjust my mind, I engaged in writing and researching characters. In the past three years, I wrote a book called "Victory Characters," changed the manuscript eight times, and made a plan for new characters, retaining the characteristics of old Chinese characters and adopting the strengths of characters from various countries. It has solved the problem of homophones in phonetic characters and the problem of dialects. It is now beginning to be published and made available to the cultural circles for research and guidance.

Twenty

Mr. Jinshan:

Thank you for your concern in asking about my "life" and "writing." I am really sorry, recently my life has been very simple. In addition to reading newspapers and magazines every day, I have simply done nothing. On the one hand, it is because the store is busy and I don't have a quiet mind to write. No! Frankly speaking, I am not qualified enough. However, I am a lover of literature and art. Although my strength is insufficient, I want to learn and dedicate my sincere heart to learning. I hope for more guidance in the future. It would be best if the "Literary Tea Party" could set up a "Literary Writing Guidance Group."

To the Literary Salon

— Lü Baihua

How could our flourishing culture lack gentlemen like you, with your elegant pursuits, even more so with such great figures; I come to boil snow and offer a long greeting, as we sit and chat, both intimate and familiar.

It was last week, I believe! Chen Chengyin invited me to the "Literary Salon." Originally, I also wanted to see Sun Fuxi and Hualin. It just so happened that I also ran into Zhao Jingshen, so I wasn't lonely after all. Although this grand occasion of the Literary Salon was my first time participating; it is said that it was established as far back as fourteen years ago, but before those fourteen years, I remember often going to Sun Fuxi's house. At that time, I was still young, and being in contact with this person who truly possessed "literature" and "art," and was also a refined recluse, moved me greatly. His large bow tie, long hair, and amiable conversation have been deeply imprinted in the heart of this weary traveler for many years and will not fade away. At that time, he ran the "Art Wind" magazine. Because of a traveler's life of moving around, my time in Shanghai was very short. Apart from occasionally writing one or two short pieces for the local supplement of this newspaper at the time, I didn't contribute to any other publications. Soon after, I arrived in Nanjing, where life was relatively stable, but the endless official documents made my head spin. I lived in "Sheepskin Lane," constantly looking out at the river embankment and sparse willows, and longing for Bai Lixi's five ramskins. In this era, how could poor scholars raise their status? So, whenever I had free time, I would pick up my pen and dispel my sorrows. The "Central Park" section of the Central Daily News, edited by Hualin, could be said to be my park for dispelling sorrows. "Baihua" was the pen name I started using to commemorate my deceased mother. The veteran writer Zong Baihua didn't take offense to it. He wrote for the supplement of the Central Daily News, and it seemed to become a parallel and non-conflicting situation. I remember once, Hualin published my article twice on the same day, using both my original name and pen name. Gradually, my original name faded away, and many friends called me by my pen name. A short story in "Art Wind" called "Moon" was also sent from Nanjing at that time. It can be said that my interest in writing had grown strong, so although I didn't participate in the Literary Salon, I formed a literary connection with its founders.

Now, fourteen years later, before the Literary Salon has been restored at the fifth floor of the Great New World, I have formed another connection with the friends of the Literary Salon; it was at the end of last year and the beginning of this year. The "Literary World" monthly magazine that I co-edited with Wei Jinzhi was discontinued because it couldn't maintain its circulation. At the same time, Guo Lanxin wanted me to edit a certain newspaper's supplement, but I left because of the fee issue. I only taught at the Zhijiang Law and Politics School and felt very bored. At this time, Lu Danlin was editing "The Beginning of Man" and invited me to help him. Perhaps it was a response to a shared desire, we got along very well. And there was Chen Chengyin, whom I had often met for many years, and still do to this day. It was from Xu Zhongnian's article that I learned that they were both key figures in the Literary Salon. I am an outsider to literature and art, and besides, in my middle-aged sorrow, I can no longer muster the courage to "write at a gallop." However, the vague past and present circumstances have made me feel a little warmth and closeness towards the Literary Salon. When I shook hands with Sun Fuxi again, he said:

"You haven't changed a bit!"

This made me smile bitterly. More than ten years have passed, how could I still be the same? There were many people there that day, and Chengyin tirelessly introduced me to them. I thought of "The gentleman's transformation is like the leopard's, his culture becomes resplendent." The "culture" of literature and art should be metaphysical in order to achieve the flourishing of moral accomplishment. "Engaging in the arts" is even more of an edict from the former sages, but the "great figure" I speak of in my poem doesn't necessarily refer to a great figure who is tall. The original meaning of the word "great" is "large." Why does literature and art stem from tea and not wine? I agree with this very much. Wine can lead to recklessness, while tea can clear the mind. Outside the passes, snow is flying. If we were to boil tea with snow water, that would be even better. See "Collection of Affairs and Literature":

"When he obtained the position of Grand Commandant, he took snow water to brew tea and said, 'Does the Dang family have this pleasure?' He replied, 'How would they know this? They can only drink lamb wine under gold-embroidered tents.'"

In this way, the friends of the Literary Salon, gathering in a "place adorned with books," will not fall into clichés. This has already been explained in the points for improvement discussed by Chen Chengyin, Hualin, and Xu Zhongnian in "Spring and Autumn" a few days ago. With a little warmth and a sense of closeness, I wrote this poem that is not very good.

Interesting Anecdotes from the Literary World

— Zhan Weng

When Chen Shiyi lived in Suzhou, he was already eighty years old. He usually regarded himself as the chief priest of the poetry circle, relying on his age to act old. Shao Tanqiu from Nanchang happened to pass through Wumen and was introduced to Chen by Jin Songcen. Chen Shiyi regarded him as a young whippersnapper and looked at him with disdain. Shao Tanqiu was furious and pointed out more than ten errors in Chen Shiyi's poetry, questioning him about them. Chen Shiyi was dumbfounded and couldn't answer. The next day was the Shangsi Festival, and there was a gathering at Black Dragon Pool in Nanjing, hosted by Cao Lianheng, Shao Yuanchong, and others, who honored Chen Shiyi as their leader. Shao Tanqiu then wrote an article titled "Poetry Calamity," severely criticizing the shamelessness of poets who promoted each other, leaving Chen Shiyi completely battered and bruised. Observers all praised Shao Tanqiu for being forthright and decisive. Chen Shiyi suffered a major blow and began to look at Shao Tanqiu with new respect, no longer daring to act old.

An Inch of Mountain, an Inch of River, an Inch of Blood 99

— Ye Jingfeng

Therefore, this kind of "five-level test" was not easy to pass. The best score required forty-eight hours. That is to say, from the first to the fifth level tests, you answered satisfactorily, with no "illogical" points, and at the same time, you could endure the torture under each test. However, it was also common to get through with some lobbying, which made the tests more lenient. For example, when Tsurumi Tama returned to China for testing this time, there was a strong figure who had already lobbied for him in advance; because Tsurumi was a top talent among the "young and vigorous faction," the society should recruit him, so after only forty-eight hours of various procedures, he completed all the "initiation ceremonies" at midnight that day.

At this time, Tsurumi had passed the five-level test and successfully became a member of the Black Dragon Society, which gave him a lot of convenience in exploring the "seven secret letters." Although he was quite excited, he was physically exhausted, and he couldn't forget the disclosure work after he obtained the "seven secret letters," so he thought of going to Ueno Park to relax, both to restore some energy and to clarify all his thoughts, in preparation for the next step of work.

Who knew that just after taking a few steps, he suddenly felt someone coming out from under the eaves of the building corner, following him. Ordinary people would not feel anything in such situations. Tsurumi had things on his mind and had received quite rigorous training. He himself was doing this kind of thing, so he could easily detect it. Moreover, it was already midnight, and there were few pedestrians on the road, so it was easy to distinguish whether the person was following him or was just an ordinary pedestrian.

However, Tsurumi was not surprised to find someone following him, only silently noting "one" in his mind. That is to say, he had discovered one tail. Little did he know that just after noting one, there were as many as five more, who came out from the dark corners on the left and right sides of the street, hanging back at a considerable distance, following closely. If it were just the Black Dragon Society sending people to complete the procedures, creating a kind of "pledge of allegiance," then to Tsurumi, it would be a childish game, and he wouldn't be flustered at all. But how could Tsurumi, who carried one, two, or even three layers of secrets, be careless?

"Are they the hounds of the Orchid Group? Are they the mortal enemies of the Black Dragon Society, the Jiangchuan Restoration Society? (Most of the members of this Restoration Society are former Tokugawa faction members, who are politically opposed to the Black Dragon Society in domestic affairs.) Or are they other emerging, hidden and dangerous enemies?" Because at this time, the war of aggression against China had already been brewing successfully; the military, political, and financial circles, filled with guys greedy for fame and wealth, had already established many factions, competing for interests abroad and power at home, or even competing for both power and interests; the factions were fighting with each other, and their subordinates had assassins, specializing in assassination work.

In normal times, let alone just five or six people, Tsurumi wouldn't have paid any attention to them. Even if there were twice as many, he would have weighed their strength first, joked with them, and then talked. You must know that Tsurumi's "judo" was close to the seventh dan, and it was usually easy to deal with ten or so people. His "kendo" had also passed the fifth dan, and he had learned from renowned kendo masters of the time, and could use special techniques to fight swords barehanded; as for marksmanship, in addition to receiving a special shooting skill award at the Army University, he had won the top prize in the military's suburban "Wumu" regiment. But why did he come to Tokyo? Why did he join the Black Dragon Society? Wasn't it all to reveal the secret of the seven secret letters of several international masterminds?

Mrs. Davis

— Eng. Henry Carr

Free Talk

Blood Plasma

— Shao Fu

The blood plasma trading case has become one of the trendy topics. In fact, just like other commodities such as automobiles, steel, aircraft equipment, military blankets, and ration food boxes, what's so different about it? However, because it is blood plasma, something fresh and bloody from inside the human body, everyone pays special attention to it.

The United States Foreign Liquidation Commission should be held responsible for this incident. First, the merchants purchased it from the Liquidation Office through formal procedures; it was neither stolen nor smuggled. Second, the blood plasma was mixed among many items; no one knew about it beforehand, and the Liquidation Office never announced a ban on trading it. Third, the term "black market" is not very appropriate here. Wholesalers bought it in large quantities, added interest, freight, and consumption costs, and then wholesaled it to various pharmacies, so naturally they had to raise the selling price. Pharmacies publicly marked the price for sale; is this a black market? Is raising the selling price a black market?

Since they sold it themselves, how can they say that the buyers are violating the law? If it is a violation, the first violator is the seller. Therefore, the most regrettable thing is that they lacked careful consideration when they first offered it for sale.

Now this batch of blood plasma has been sealed up, and those who engaged in this profitable business are truly left between tears and laughter. They now say that as long as there is a reasonable price, it can be confiscated. Although there will be losses, there is nothing they can do. They hope that the organizers can consider this solution; otherwise, merchants will not dare to trade with the Liquidation Commission in the future.

A Rapid, Decisive, and Painful Primitive Judicial Performance: A Report from a Foreign Reporter

— Liaoran

American news reporter William Ayers once lived in the Communist Party's "Liberated Area" in Shandong for some time. After returning to Shanghai, he recently published several articles in the English-language "China Press" in this city, recalling the "Liberated Area." The most interesting article is the one that describes how the Communists handled Han Hao. Because from this article, we can see how the Communist Party's judicial organs are organized, how they exercise their powers, and what the Communist Party's judicial concepts are. The article begins by describing the peace and tranquility of the Chinese countryside. Suddenly, the article writes: "Secretary Hu said, 'There will be a mass meeting this afternoon...we are going to execute someone.'" The matter is so sudden, and the local number one traitor is going to be tried. There was no prior announcement or news coverage; the leaders of various groups firmly organized by the Communist regime received the following order: "Select a number of people from your group to attend the meeting this afternoon." There was no preliminary hearing, which is in stark contrast to the International Military Tribunal's trial of Japanese war criminals.

At that time, Secretary Hu told me (the reporter said himself) that the traitor did not have to be punished as long as he repented and admitted his crimes. In the following twenty-four hours, the Communists I spoke with all agreed that if the traitor was willing to repent, he would not have to be tried. Every time I heard this sentence, I felt a chill, because this sentence had religious overtones, which is contrary to the Communist Party's materialism. ... The Communist Eighth Route Army's method of dealing with prisoners is to educate them through persuasion, first denouncing the other side's war as wrong, then praising the Communist Party, and finally declaring to him personally that peace is more valuable than war. A persuaded prisoner who expresses repentance and a determination to reform can be released, otherwise he must be tried. The traitor being tried this time, Wang, had been subjected to a year of persuasive education by the Eighth Route Army and did not repent, so he was to be "tried" on August 31. Here, the term "trial" is similar in name but different in reality to the "trial" used by the International Military Tribunal for Japanese war criminals. The trial of the traitor Wang can actually be said to have been held privately during that year of persuasive education. The trial that I witnessed that day was merely the pronouncement of the verdict. There was no need to guess how the verdict would be decided; everyone already knew the outcome.

Plot

About two thousand people were seated. On the platform, the curtain was drawn back, and Judge Sun sat in the center, with two judges from the "government" on his right, and three judges sent by people's organizations. On his left were Prosecutor Yu and the clerk. When the prisoner's truck arrived, there was a moment of silence in the courtroom...I was reminded of the drumbeats of the French Revolution. The truck stopped in front of the stage, and two police officers escorted the prisoner, whose hands were tied behind his back. ... The prosecutor began his argument. ... After the prosecutor finished his argument ... Judge Sun asked, "Is the argument fair?" The defendant replied, "Some parts are wrong, some things I didn't do." Question: "Do you want to call witnesses?" Answer: "I dare not trouble the court." Then Judge Sun walked towards the crowd gathered, asking what opinions the people had. No one spoke. "According to the crimes committed by the defendant, he should be sentenced to death. The court has already discussed this. What are your opinions?" Judge Sun waved his fist in the air and shouted loudly: "Kill!" Then the crowd also shouted three times: "Kill! Kill! Kill!" Judge Sun took out the written verdict from his pocket and read it line by line..." After reading it, the defendant was shot on the spot. (The full text can be found in the English-language "China Press" on November 14)

From the facts observed above, the Communist Party's judicial concept is primitive, backward, and barbaric; it is ten thousand miles away from the modern democratic judicial concept. According to the principles of democratic politics, judicial power and administrative power are separate, but the Communist Party's judiciary is purely an appendage of administration. Communist law has no so-called "principle of legality," and guilt is not necessarily punishable; as long as one is willing to "repent," they can be declared innocent; if one is not willing to repent, they must be sentenced to death. Therefore, the entire Communist judiciary can be encompassed by just eight words: "Those who obey me will live, those who defy me will die." The Communist judiciary is merely a means to achieve political goals; it has no independence in itself. Therefore, as long as the political goal is achieved, the judicial means can be dispensed with, and the defendant will naturally be acquitted. The judges have people's representatives, which seems to be a jury system, but the people's representatives under the Communist Party's rule are nominal only. Even the "masses" attending the meeting are selected by the "group leaders" who are ordered from above to send a certain number of people to participate, so it is easy to imagine that the people's representatives sitting on the jury are even more chosen by the Communist Party themselves. Even if they were truly elected by the people, their task would probably only be to sit on the jury. If the presiding judge did not shout "Kill!", they probably wouldn't dare to shout "Kill!" first. Don't you see those "masses"? When the judge asked them what their opinions were, no one spoke until the judge waved his fist and shouted "Kill!" Then they followed with "Kill! Kill! Kill!" This shows that these jurors are used to deceive the people; their opinions cannot affect the verdict at all. Furthermore, the judicial organ represents the state in exercising criminal penalties, and has the right to punish those who should be held criminally responsible according to the law, without seeking the opinions of the general public; but the Communist judiciary is not like that. On the one hand, the verdict is already known to everyone, and the judge has already written the verdict and put it in his pocket, but on the other hand, he hypocritically asks the masses how to punish the defendant. While their hypocrisy is laughable, this action is completely incompatible with the modern judicial concept!

Conclusion

The Communist judiciary has no appellate system, no so-called appeals, reversals, complaints, or retrials. Once a verdict is made, it is immediately executed. Even if the verdict is improper, there is no way to remedy it. There is also no preliminary hearing, and the trial procedure is very simple. There is only accusation, no defense. The defendant who appears in court is not only physically restricted (wearing handcuffs), but there is also no one familiar with the law to defend him. Therefore, a case can be concluded in just a few tens of minutes, which is vastly different from the year it took to try German war criminals in Nuremberg, the half a year it has taken to try Japanese war criminals in Tokyo, and the several months it takes the Shanghai High Court to try each case of traitors. Modern democratic countries all recognize that the purpose of punishment is to prevent crime, and criminal litigation adopts the principle of discovering the truth in substance. Therefore, the defendant is presumed innocent until proven guilty, and evidence favorable or unfavorable to the defendant should be investigated, and the defendant should be given the opportunity to defend himself and the opportunity to make a final statement. The Communist Party believes that the judiciary is the superstructure, and the purpose of punishment is nothing more than to eliminate obstacles to communism, so all so-called trial levels, defenses, investigations, and preliminary hearings are naturally absent. This kind of judiciary is far removed from the judicial concepts of modern democratic countries, so it can be said to be a primitive, backward, and barbaric judicial concept.

Thoughts from a Photograph

— Zuiyin

No matter how ferocious a person is, when taking a photograph, they always like to have a bit of a smile; even if they don't smile, they have to put on a kind of look that gives people a good impression of them. The reason why humans are different from animals is probably because they have a bit more of this.

Therefore, when you look at those different faces in the windows of photo studios, you always feel that those people are all "good people," all "smiling," and all powerful and capable; no one lets their ferocious true nature show in the lens. It is said that this is certainly a form of hypocrisy, but social order depends on this for maintenance.

These words were spoken to me by someone not long ago. At the time, I only felt that the person who said these words had quite a bit of insight. Unexpectedly, today, I feel more and more that the above in-depth and simple explanation is truly reasonable!

Look: from Goering in Germany to Zhou Fohai in China, these executioners of war and accomplices of the invaders, when they arrive in court, all give eloquent speeches, talking a blue streak. If we were a little naive, we would really feel that this world is full of benevolent and moral people. Before committing suicide, Goering even tried to transmit his will, speaking grandly about "the future mission of the German people," etc. It's not that he didn't realize he was wrong until his death; he certainly knew that he had been acting recklessly in the past, but even though he understood it in his heart, he couldn't help but "sacredly" talk nonsense, trying to make people see him as a revolutionary. Furthermore, even a heinous traitor like Zhou Fohai had to come up with a set of nonsensical defenses. Fortunately, the men and women compatriots in Shanghai and many other occupied areas are still alive and not too forgetful, otherwise Zhou Fohai's "simply stirring the hearts of the people" eloquent talk would have really plunged us into confusion!

What I find most interesting is that since the public trial of the number one traitor, Chen Gongbo, all the traitors, big and small, have been self-confessed, speaking plausibly. Among them, some knew that they could hardly escape death, but they still refused to be frank and all unanimously released their words, making a "solemn" appearance. This is just like: when taking a photo, even a great bandit who has murdered countless people shows a bit of amiable and friendly appearance.

In this day and age, no one reveals their heart anymore. Everyone is acting in a nonsensical play! No wonder, a few years ago, a baby in Manila died three days later because its heart grew outside its chest!

Private Life

— Saint

Starting with Xia Peizhen

Fifteen years ago, Xia Peizhen, a female movie star second only to Hu Die, was sentenced to one year in prison after the victory because of a drug case. According to reports, she will soon be released. This news reminded me of the past of this red star who once starred in "The Burning of the Red Lotus Temple."

In 1932, she joined the Star Film Company and began her life as a movie star in that golden age. At that time, her income, in addition to remuneration, was two hundred yuan per month, and the price for filming one day was seventy yuan. This kind of generous treatment was not comparable to that of ordinary people, but she was therefore infected with the worst habit in the world. Her artistic achievements were destroyed at this point. Later, when she left the Star Film Company, she had a legal dispute with the company, and for a time, she was a well-known news figure. However, the flood-like poison gradually eroded her youth and vitality, leading to a complete collapse, and she rarely performed in the film industry. When we recall this history of an artist's downfall, we also feel endless regret! Reality is always the most ruthless! From Xia Peizhen, I thought of Yuan Meiyun. This pair of red stars with black records both committed the same mistake. Their future was ruined by "one wrong step," and now there is no way to fix it!

The difficult lives of film and drama artists make us sympathize, but precisely because of working day and night and having a freer lifestyle, their private lives are most likely to collapse. A moment's difference in thought can lead to irremediable consequences; I am not preaching here, but as an artist, you must be able to manage yourself and restrain yourself. Otherwise, once emotions are released without restraint, then the Sentimental behaviors of depravity, corruption, and committing suicide will kill individuals and kill art!

Sanmao

— Zhang Leping

The Story of the Psychoanalyst

The Innocent Murderer 9

— Xu Gui

After a while, he heard the people below shouting loudly. He knew he must be almost at the top. He opened his eyes and saw Lu Xun standing against the wall, about ten feet away from him. Mi En felt a little dizzy, but not afraid at all. Instead, he felt happy. He said, "Lu Xun!"

"I'm Mi En."

"Hmm."

"I've come here to accompany you down. Don't worry, I'll take care of you."

Lu Xun moved slightly, but did not turn around. He said, "I can only do it. I know people will misunderstand me. But I can't care about so much."

"I had a hard time climbing up," Mi En said happily. "How did you get here anyway?"

Lu Xun said, "I hit him on the head and then shot her. His head didn't break as a result. The stick actually bounced out, hurting my hand so much."

"It doesn't matter," Mi En said. "Everything is over now."

Lu Xun suddenly raised his head and cried out in a special tone: "Mi En!"

"Oh, Lu Xun!"

Lu Xun paused for a long time, then asked, "Do you think doing this will hurt her heart?"

Mi En replied, "Good heavens! She definitely won't be hurt. She definitely won't feel anything."

"Do you think that's definitely how it is?" Lu Xun asked with a strange laugh.

"I think that's definitely how it is."

"That's great. Then, let's go down now." He stood up immediately.

"Mi En shouted," Stop! Don't do it!" But Lu Xun took a pistol and fired at his own head. With a loud bang, blood splattered, and his body slowly leaned down and lay down.

Mi En listlessly stared at his body, still leaning against the ladder. The people below all shouted. He closed his eyes tightly and cried, "It's all right, there's no need to worry now."

When Mi En got into the car, the police officer handed him a letter, but he didn't have the heart to pay attention to it at all. He simply forgot about it. It wasn't until he got home that he felt it in his pocket and remembered it.

The envelope was addressed to Dr. Mi En. Mi En read it and felt very sad, but it was clearly not written by Lu Xun later. He tore open the envelope and looked at it. Inside were four one-pound notes and two ten-shilling notes.

He looked at it for a long time before putting it back in the envelope, putting it in his pocket, and then closing his eyes. He didn't sleep all night, of course he was so tired that he couldn't support himself anymore. (The End)

News Figure: Overseas Chinese Tycoon Hu Wenhu

— Jifeng

Hu Wenhu has arrived in Shanghai.

According to Mr. Hu himself, the purpose of this trip is to pay his respects to Chairman Chiang in Nanjing and report on the situation of overseas Chinese. In addition, he will also visit President Song and Minister Yu to seek guidance on the future development of the Fujian Economic Construction Company, which Mr. Hu presides over.

Some newspapers have reported that Mr. Hu enjoys Peking opera and various local operas, and that he even went to the Gong Wu Tai theater to see a stage production with elaborate sets. One evening newspaper even ran the headline: "Hu Wenhu Watches 'Seven Swords and Thirteen Knights'," serving as a free advertisement for Gong Wu Tai.

Thinking of Tiger Balm

Mentioning Hu Wenhu immediately brings to mind the Tiger Balm, Ba Feng Dan, and Headache Powder produced by Tiger Balm Eng Aun Tong. "Tiger Balm Eng Aun Tong" was built up single-handedly by Mr. Hu. Currently, branches are located in Rangoon, Java, Siam, Hong Kong, Guangzhou, Shanghai, Hankou, Fuzhou, Tianjin, and other places. There are also special agency offices in major ports around the world. The success of its sales is truly remarkable. However, it is said that when Mr. Hu first started the business, he personally led his employees, dressed in colorful tiger costumes, to promote and peddle their products to various businesses, struggling to get by. Eventually, he built his fortune and became a prominent overseas merchant, which was no easy feat.

Mr. Hu's ancestral home is Yongding, Fujian, but he was born in Rangoon, Burma. Fifty years before the founding of the Republic of China, his father took a "big-eyed chicken boat" (named so because the sailboats of that time had two large eyes painted on the bow) and went to Rangoon alone to establish Eng Aun Tong Chinese medicine shop, marrying a woman named Li, who gave birth to two sons, Wenhu and Wenbao.

Mr. Hu is known for his generosity and actively supports general charitable and public welfare causes, making him incomparable to those who are merely "charity swindlers." He has initiated the establishment of secondary schools in various overseas locations to provide educational opportunities for the children of overseas Chinese, as well as building hospitals and constructing nursing homes, benefiting the overseas Chinese community significantly. It is worth noting Mr. Hu's concern for national affairs: during the Northern Expedition, the September 18th Incident, and the January 28th Incident, he invariably donated large sums of money and significant quantities of medicine. During the War of Resistance, he also provided considerable assistance to the motherland.

Starting a Newspaper in Shanghai

Some say that Mr. Hu's visit to Shanghai this time is to prepare for the publication of a Shanghai edition of the Sing Tao newspaper series, to be called "Sing Tao Shanghai Daily," because Mr. Hu's expansion into media ventures is "significant..." Hu Wenhu's newspaper publishing venture began in 1921 when he invested 50,000 yuan in Rangoon to establish the Burma Morning Post. In 1928, he invested 500,000 yuan in Singapore to establish the Sin Chew Daily, dominating the Singaporean newspaper industry. In 1931, he also established the Sing Hua Daily in Shantou. With Mr. Hu's strong financial resources, his newspaper publishing ventures certainly have a good chance of success.

In the Pasir Panjang area of Singapore, Mr. Hu once single-handedly opened a large-scale swimming pool. The pool also included basketball courts, tennis courts, badminton courts, and other facilities. A sign hung at the entrance read, "Only Chinese are allowed to swim inside." This, compared with the sign "Dogs and Chinese not allowed" in Shanghai in the past, truly reflects the changes that have taken place.

Mr. Hu is now sixty-four years old, but his spirit is as strong as ever. It seems he still has several more ventures to complete!
